A duck walks into a pharmacy and asks the pharmacist for some  chapstick.  The pharmacist says, `Will that be cash or charge.`  The duck says, `Neither. Just put it on my bill.`  _ Jennifer Stevens, Foster's Daily Democrat, New Hampshire  A man walks into a bar, and there's a genie lamp on the counter near a really small man playing a really small piano. The guy picks up the lamp, rubs it and a genie pops out.  ``I will grant you any wish,'' the genie says.  ``Well, the man says, ``I want a million bucks.''  ``It is done,'' the genie says and disappears into his lamp.  Then poof, the bar is filled with hundreds and hundreds of ducks flying.  ``I said BUCKS,'' he said.  ``Yeah, that genie's hard of hearing,'' said the bartender. ``Do you think I really asked for a 12-inch pianist?''  _ Kristen Cook  INSECT LOGIC  Little Billy was left to fix lunch. When his mother returned with a friend, she noticed that Billy had already strained his tea.  ``Did you find the tea strainer?' his mother asked.  ``No, mother, I couldn't find it, so I used a fly swatter,'' replied Billy. His mother nearly fainted, so Billy hastily added, ``Don't get excited, mother I used an old one.''  _ Chris Feist, Waco, Texas  REPTILIAN ROADHOG  We were driving along Route 7 through Connecticut on our way to Tanglewood for a Sunday show. It was a peaceful section of country road, but we were waved to a sudden stop by a man and a woman, each standing on one side of the yellow line.  What was wrong? Were they in trouble? Should we call somebody? We waited politely. A car pulled up behind us. Then another car. Through the rear window I could see still more cars.  The couple continued to hold up their hands authoritatively. Car horns began to be tapped. Others, it seemed, might be a bit late for Tanglewood. The couple continued to hold up their hands. Local police in very plain clothes?  I started to get out to ask. The woman shook her head and pointed to the road.  We watched. A turtle, deliberate and dignified, crawled across the road and headed for the ditch on the other side.  _ Janet Mansbridge (Reported in N.Y. Times Metropolitan Diary)  GOOD DAY, WIRE WANDERERS!!!!! Just put it on my bill.`  _ Jennifer Stevens, Foster's Daily Democrat, New Hampshire  A man walks into a bar, and there's a genie lamp on the counter near a really small man playing a really small piano. The guy picks up the lamp, rubs it and a genie pops out.  ``I will grant you any wish,'' the genie says.  ``Well, the man says, ``I want a million bucks.''  ``It is done,'' the genie says and disappears into his lamp.  Then poof, the bar is filled with hundreds and hundreds of ducks flying.  ``I said BUCKS,'' he said.  ``Yeah, that genie's hard of hearing,'' said the bartender. ``Do you think I really asked for a 12-inch pianist?''  _ Kristen Cook  INSECT LOGIC  Little Billy was left to fix lunch. When his mother returned with a friend, she noticed that Billy had already strained his tea.  ``Did you find the tea strainer?' his mother asked.  ``No, mother, I couldn't find it, so I used a fly swatter,'' replied Billy. His mother nearly fainted, so Billy hastily added, ``Don't get excited, mother I used an old one.''  _ Chris Feist, Waco, Texas  REPTILIAN ROADHOG  We were driving along Route 7 through Connecticut on our way to Tanglewood for a Sunday show. It was a peaceful section of country road, but we were waved to a sudden stop by a man and a woman, each standing on one side of the yellow line.  What was wrong? Were they in trouble? Should we call somebody? We waited politely. A car pulled up behind us. Then another car. Through the rear window I could see still more cars.  The couple continued to hold up their hands authoritatively. Car horns began to be tapped. Others, it seemed, might be a bit late for Tanglewood. The couple continued to hold up their hands. Local police in very plain clothes?  I started to get out to ask. The woman shook her head and pointed to the road.  We watched. A turtle, deliberate and dignified, crawled across the road and headed for the ditch on the other side.  _ Janet Mansbridge (Reported in N.Y. Times Metropolitan Diary)  GOOD DAY, WIRE WANDERERS!!!!! POLITICAL BRIEFS: THE CAMPAIGNS AND THE ISSUES (bl) By MICHAEL WINES c.1996 N.Y. Times News Service  MINNESOTA: GOP Focusing On Welfare Vote  Minnesota Republicans profess delight over Thursday's Senate vote to enact a stiff new welfare law, not so much because their party's proposal passed but because one Democrat made a point of opposing it.  That Democrat is Sen. Paul Wellstone, the energetic and sometimes hyperbolic Minnesotan who is locked in a potentially tight re-election struggle against the man he unseated in 1990, Rudy Boschwitz.  Boschwitz has run a buzzsaw of a campaign featuring billboards that dubbed his opponent ``Senator Welfare'' and ``Welfare Man'' for his support of federal antipoverty programs, and he seems poised to push the issue even harder.  ``The vast majority of Minnesotans want workfare, not welfare, and Paul Wellstone is arrogantly disregarding the wishes of the people of Minnesota,'' said Jon Lerner, the manager of the Boschwitz campaign.  Wellstone, who gave a fiery Senate speech Thursday mocking the notion that cuts in welfare spending were reforms, was unrepentant. In an interview Friday, he said the evidence ``is irrefutable that this legislation will create more poverty and more hunger among people.''  ``There can be a zillion attacks,'' he said. ``I won't vote to take food out of the mouths of hungry children.''  Boschwitz's campaign could use a lift. Many polls say Wellstone has a lead of 6 to 8 points despite Republican attacks.  A former Republican pollster in Minneapolis, Bill Morris, said Wellstone's decision to buck both the Senate and his President on the welfare bill could hurt.  Belying its liberal image, Minnesota takes a moderate stand on welfare and has grown more conservative in the 1990s, Morris said.  Then again, Morris said, one of Wellstone's main assets is his charismatic image as a go-getter and a maverick.  Wellstone solidified that image Thursday, promising a national tour of poverty-stricken areas to underscore the need for government aid.  Friday, Boschwitz announced his own tour _ this one, Lerner said, highlighting ``people who have worked hard and overcome adversity and lifted themselves up from the welfare rolls.''  CALIFORNIA: Change Advocates In Bitter Dispute  In the prohibition movement known as campaign finance reform, it is as if war had broken out between the Salvation Army and the Women's Christian Temperance Union.  The California Public Interest Research Group, part of Ralph Nader's political muckraking network, is locked in a ferocious battle with Common Cause, the League of Women Voters and their allies over whose November ballot initiative will best clean up state political campaigns.  The fray began after talks on a single ballot measure limiting campaign contributions broke down between Calpirg, as the Nader group is known, and an umbrella group led by Common Cause, the League, the American Association of Retired Persons and United We Stand America.  The two groups wrote separate proposals and gathered enough signatures to put them on the ballot.  But they did not stop arguing. Calpirg's measure, it seemed, contained a ``poison pill'' specifying that if both initiatives passed, the Common Cause plan would be nullified if it drew fewer votes, but the Calpirg measure would take effect regardless. Common Cause counterattacked, seeking to bounce Calpirg off the ballot.  When that failed, the Common Cause group sued to force the Calpirg measure off the ballot, arguing that its signers were never told of a fine-print clause that would repeal all limits on gifts to state officials. The court rejected the suit. Common Cause and its allies have appealed.  Meanwhile, Calpirg countersued after California's attorney general added the gift-law repeal to the combined ballot measure that voters will see in November.  The rivals call it a philosophical dispute. But Tony Miller, a Common Cause board member and head of the umbrella group, also calls the Calpirg proposal ``outrageous'' and a loophole-filled ``deception.''  Doug Phelps, who heads both the national Nader group and the California initiative, said Common Cause and its allies ``are not willing to debate the real issue,'' containing spending, but want only to tinker with the current system.  It is the sort of guerrilla fight one expects to see in a nasty political campaign, not a movement for change. ``The way this is going,'' Phelps said, ``the public-interest community in California is going to be on Oprah Winfrey by October.''  FLORIDA: Tumbling Off Spending Wagon  It once seemed that the political temperance movement had succeeded in Florida. But campaigns are once again off the spending wagon there, too.  The state Legislature had enacted a law providing public financing for the campaigns of candidates for governor and the state cabinet, matching all private contributions up to $250 as long as candidates agreed to limit overall spending.  The law worked as planned in 1994, when the state put up $11.4 million to match contributions for statewide candidates, including $4.7 million that aided the paper-thin victory of Gov. Lawton Chiles, a Democrat.  But then a raft of unhappy Republicans and some Democrats, calling the law ``welfare for politicians,'' mounted a petition drive to place on the ballot a constitutional amendment that would abolish the law.  And the Legislature decided this year not to replenish the fund that provides the matching money.  The petition drive has been suspended. ``We think the funding source has been eradicated,'' said Phil Handy, a Winter Park businessman. Many expect the issue to be settled, eventually, in the courts. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19960804.0058 type=story > <HEADLINE> CONGRESSIONAL REPUBLICANS DISTANCE THEMSELVES FROM DOLE </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> WASHINGTON (BC-REPUBS-DOLE-750-(2TAKES)-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199608.gz"

(To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Jane Curtin in a scene from ``3rd Rock from the Sun.'' By PETER MARKS c.1996 The New York Times (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  Jane Curtin was cleaning her kitchen in Connecticut one frigid January evening when the telephone rang.  It was her old friend, the comedy writer Bonnie Turner. The call was not spur of the moment. This was a television emergency. Would Curtin, she wanted to know, consider another go at a sitcom?  Turner, it seemed, was in a bind. She and her husband, Terry, writing partners with whom Curtin had worked on ``Saturday Night Live'' and the 1993 film ``The Coneheads,'' were developing a new comedy about a group of eccentric aliens who come to Earth and take human form.  The writers urgently required a straight woman with a bit of an edge to play the aliens' thoroughly human foil, and Curtin sprang to mind.  ``They'd shot the pilot, and the character that they created for the love interest didn't quite work,'' Curtin says. ``They had made this character too sweet, and so there was no tension.''  Although Curtin said she was committed to another project and was not available, Turner was not put off. She told Curtin she could have the role on her own terms.  ``She said, `You only have to do as many shows as you want,''' the actress recalls. ``I said, `OK, seven.' And she said, `Seven it is.'''  As effortlessly as that, Curtin began her re-entry into a universe she had already conquered in five seasons on ``Kate and Allie,'' the successful 1980s sitcom in which she was a co-star with Susan Saint James.  The new show, ``3rd Rock From the Sun,'' which also stars John Lithgow _ who won a 1996 Emmy for his sitcom debut in this role _ would prove to be another hit for Curtin, an actress who seems unable to miss on series television.  One of the original Not Ready for Prime Time Players on ``Saturday Night Live,'' on which she appeared for five seasons ending in 1980, Curtin was already television-comedy royalty when she signed on to ``Kate and Allie'' in 1984.  ``3rd Rock,'' which had its premiere on NBC in January 1996, completes the actress' comedic hat trick. In The New York Times, the television critic John J. O'Connor called the show ``brilliantly idiotic, a daffy pastiche of farce, burlesque and satire.''  Of all the original ``Saturday Night Live'' cast members, Curtin has had by far the most successful television career. Others who joined ``Saturday Night'' in subsequent years, such as Bill Murray and Eddie Murphy, went on to film stardom or graduated to standout roles in prime time, as did Julia-Louis Dreyfus and Phil Hartman.  But no other ``Saturday Night'' alum can match Curtin for such sustained achievement on acclaimed shows.  ``There's really no formula to this _ you just get lucky,'' Curtin says by telephone from her dressing room in a television studio in Los Angeles, where ``3rd Rock From the Sun'' is taped.  ``I was always hoping for a life in television, but I didn't know what to imagine after `Saturday Night Live,''' she says. ``I had done a movie while I was on `Saturday Night,' and I had done theater before that, but I really liked television.  ``I liked the immediacy of it, the fact that you didn't have to repeat yourself every week and that it was fast,'' she says. ``Ideally, a sitcom is like a little play each week, and I liked that instant gratification.''  On ``3rd Rock From the Sun,'' Curtin plays Dr. Mary Albright, a professor at a Midwestern college who shares an office with Dick Solomon, the human identity that Lithgow's alien character has assumed.  The show revels in its own silliness. Much of the sitcom's humor derives from the absurdities of everyday life as revealed by a family of aliens who are entirely unfamiliar with the mores of expected behavior.  Albright may be a scholar, but she's a little thick when it comes to the bizarre goings-on in the Solomon household, populated by Lithgow and his compadres from outer space, played by Kristen Johnson, French Stewart and Joseph Gordon-Levitt.  It may be that she is blinded by her attraction to Lithgow's character. In any event, she is the oblivious/suspicious human bystander, a role with a great tradition in supernatural sitcoms, from Gladys Kravitz, the nosy neighbor in ``Bewitched,'' to Dr. Bellows, the snooty superior officer in ``I Dream of Jeanie.''  Curtin laughs easily about her exploits on ``3rd Rock.'' She takes the show no more seriously than do viewers.  ``It's actually about nothing,'' the actress says, a characterization that in the age of ``Seinfeld'' is a compliment. ``It's just about the way human beings behave.  ``I loved the character, but I also loved the sense of humor on the show,'' she says. ``I had known John in New York, and I'm very comfortable with him. It made me laugh when John and I were working together _ there just would be puddles on the floor.''  Eventually, Curtin agreed to extend her limited commitment to the show. In July, she and her family, diehard Easterners, moved to Los Angeles.  ``We decided to give it a go,'' she says, adding that her daughter is attending high school there.  A semblance of normalcy amid the insanity of weekly television is important to Curtin. One of the things she loved most about ``Kate and Allie,'' she says, was that she was able to walk from her apartment in Manhattan to the studio in the Ed Sullivan Theater, where the show was taped and where David Letterman now performs nightly.  Curtin is not a flashy performer. Unlike many who made their names on ``Saturday Night Live,'' she had no quintessential Jane Curtin character, no Church Lady or Buckwheat or Samurai Dry Cleaner. Curtin made an art of fitting in wherever she was needed, a talent that has served her well through the years.  With continued television success has come a comfort level with her abilities and a confidence that there will always be a place for her.  ``It's gotten to the point where if it wasn't going to be fun, I didn't want to do it,'' she says of the job offers she has turned down. ``If it wasn't going to be right or you aren't interested in the people involved, you should be comfortable enough not to be intimidated into doing it.''  At the moment, she is plenty comfortable. The 20-minute commute from home to studio agrees with her, as does life in California, to her surprise.  ``I keep trying to tell friends of mine in New York who are theater people or movie people what it's like to do a sitcom,'' she says. ``I paint a very rosy picture. And I don't think they believe me.''  (Peter Marks reports on entertainment for The New York Times.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) ------------------------------------------------- </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19970113.0214 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MONITORING-TV-FOR-KIDS-PAGEUP-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> IS TV ONLY FOR DESSERT? A PARENTS' GUIDE TO SELECTIVE FAMILY VIDEO VIEWING (To publish this ``separate buy'' article it must be purchased -- the rate is not prohibitive -- from one of these New York Times Syndicate sales representatives: (--U.S., Canada and the Pacific: CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City, 816-822-8448 or fax her at 816-822-1444. (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (--Mexico, Central America, South America: PAUL FINCH in Los Angeles, 310-996-0075; fax, 310-996-0089. Excerpted from the book ``Stay Tuned,'' published by Doubleday, a division of Bantam Doubleday Dell Publishing Group, Inc. Includes a 100-word sidebar. By JANE MURPHY and KAREN TUCKER c.1996 by Jane Murphy and Karen Tucker World English language rights only. (Distributed by New York Times Syndicate.)  Video can be an entertaining reward and a valuable educational resource when parents get involved in their kids' viewing. But the question of what to watch is perhaps the most highly charged area, because it involves the hot topics of violence, sex, language, stereotypes and negative role models.  ``As a rule, a VCR should be used to watch programs, not to `watch TV,''' say Jane Murphy and Karen Tucker, authors of ``Stay Tuned'' (Doubleday, 1996), a book on responsible family TV viewing. Select what you want to see, watch it, then turn off the set. And consider locating your TV centrally, so it's obvious when and what the kids are watching.  Following is an excerpt from ``Stay Tuned'' on how parents can optimize the quality of their children's TV time. VIOLENCE  Over 1,000 studies have shown connections between violence in the media and violent behavior by children.  ``Children do what they see,'' says Dr. William Dietz, a researcher in the field of TV violence and its effects on children. He goes on to say that children watch approximately 12,000 violent acts per year on TV.  Most major professional organizations of educators and doctors have recommended that parents limit their children's viewing of violence because of the evidence that viewing violence is strongly linked with aggressive behavior.  One-third of kids ages 10 through 16 polled in Los Angeles in 1992 said they often want to try things they see on television. In 1993, the Yich Youth Monitor surveyed kids ages 9-17, and two-thirds of them thought there was too much violence on TV.  The American Psychological Association encourages parents to protect kids from feeling that the world is unsafe, from becoming insensitive to the pain and suffering of others, imitating negative things they see. Limiting what and how much TV our kids see is one very effective way to work toward these recommendations.  Realistically, it's impossible to screen out every bit of violence that's portrayed on the screen. And it's probably not a good idea to do that anyway, because violence is part of our human condition.  The issue and decisions around it involve your particular kid, the context and presentation of violence and whether your child is watching alone or with you to share the experience and offer commentary. SEX  We've come a long way from ``I Love Lucy,'' in which Ricky and Lucy slept in twin beds, and Lucy couldn't even use the word ``pregnant,'' which she clearly was at the time.  The American Psychological Association estimates that American teenagers are exposed to 14,000 sexual references and innuendos per year. Sex is on the soaps, in commercials and in promos for upcoming shows. Kids can easily get the idea from television and movies that sex is the dominant aspect of relationships and one they should sample soon.  Every parent has his or her own point of view regarding the presentation of sex. However you feel, you need to clarify your own feelings first, then you will be confident in presenting them to your kids. LANGUAGE  Another touchy area, language, may reflect a character's point of view, an historical context or a cultural bias. We should sensitize our kids to its impact. And, we should be aware that if we use a fair amount of ``rich'' language at home, it will be tough to rule out some programming on that basis alone.  In fact, cursing language once considered aberrant by mental health professionals is no longer used as a diagnostic criterion for certain emotional disorders.  Dr. Timothy Jay, in ``Cursing in America'' (Benjamins Publishing, 1992), writes that while dialogue in 1940s gangster movies was limited to ``You dirty rat,'' ``GoodFellas'' (1990) had 400 curse words. STEREOTYPES AND NEGATIVE ROLE MODELS  TV and video are full of presentations of characters stereotyped by gender, ethnicity or age. The references may be subtle enough to elude us, or our own biases may filter our awareness.  It's important to be alert to stereotypes and to consider whenever they are truly inappropriate for our kids or whether they represent an opportunity for discussion.  Characters in film and television sometimes act in ways you wouldn't want your kids to imitate. Cheating, drug and alcohol abuse and disrespect of teachers and parents are frequently portrayed.  How the plot gets played out is key when these issues arise. Are there negative consequences for these types of behavior?  The mix of what your kids watch is also important, because an occasional example of drug abuse may well be counterbalanced by other viewing. Of course, your kids' ages are significant indicators as well. SCARINESS  Next to violence and sex, scariness might seem like small potatoes. Truth is, the scariness of dark closets, monsters and things that go bump in the night is another big issue to tackle in selecting appropriate viewing for your kids. And it's hardly ever an obvious decision.  Certainly, age and development can provide guidelines for what are classic concerns at various stages, but what constitutes ``scary'' really comes down to the individual child and what they may be worrying about at the time.  Often you can judge ``not scary,'' ``OK scary'' or ``too scary'' by whether the scene, circumstance or character in question are cartoonish and exaggerated or too real for comfort. Some kids don't mind shoot-em-up action-adventures but can get worked up over real-life situations that are too close to home.  For instance, for a young child who's anxious about his mom who's away at work or visiting relatives even ``Bambi'' could be upsetting because Bambi's mother dies in a fire.  In ``The Uses of Enchantment'' (Random House, 1989), celebrated child psychologist Bruno Bettelheim's book on the value of fairy tales, we learn that ``scary'' may have a rightful place in children's lives. Bettelheim maintains fears and aggressions children experience as part of their normal development get confronted and resolved through these classic stories. The predicaments of Hansel and Gretel, Little Red Riding Hood and a cast of thousands of other familiar characters help kids feel less alone about their own feelings.  Of course, this point of view doesn't give us license to let the kids watch everything. Reading or hearing stories are less graphic than seeing the same themes treated on the screen, so care must be taken. HOSTING OTHER CHILDREN  These colorful gray areas are factors that must also be considered when you have other children over, so you need to be sensitive to what they're viewing.  If you have any doubt about whether the programming is right for them or OK with their parents, check ahead of time to get the green light. In fact, if you're planning a party or another occasion where kids will be watching a tape, poll the kids ahead time on what they'd like to see and get clearance and ideas from parents. Everyone, including the kids, will appreciate this consideration, and your kids' friends and their parents may reciprocate in the future. THE GRAY AREAS ARE NEVER BLACK OR WHITE  It's impossible to be a purist in making choices in these ubiquitous gray areas. Many wonderful films and programs succeed in carrying a valuable message, presenting an engaging story or offering great entertainment, except for one scene, one objectioharacter or stereotype.  For example, ``Old Yeller'' (1957) is a classic heartwarming story about a family dog and a boy's growing responsibility. There are, however, some inappropriate references made to Native Americans in this story, which is set in the 1860s American West, and the dog gets rabies and must be killed by the owner, who loves him. What do you do?  You need to make these decisions based on your knowledge of your own child. If you feel your kid can handle the dog dying, go ahead and let him watch, and discuss the fact that this movie presents some outmoded and unfair comments about America's original inhabitants.  A technology you can consider for restricting access to selected programs is the ``V-chip,'' which would censor violent programs. Parents could program their sets to not show violent programs (indicated as violent in TV listings).  A drawback to the chip is that someone somewhere will predetermine for your family what's considered too violent. The Motion Picture Association of America (MPAA) ratings of theatrical movies are often steeped in controversy, because one person's PG may be another's PG-13.  Perhaps the best way to handle TV programming is drawing up your own criteria and advising advertisers of your support for government and industry guarantees of programming designed specifically for children.  Begin your deliberations by considering your kids' ages, maturity, concerns and personalities. These will be the factors that will contribute the most to your making the right decisions for them. -0- SIDEBAR: WHAT ARE OUR KIDS WATCHING?  Here are some thought-provoking facts to consider when evaluating what your child watches on TV:  _ The average child grows up watching 20,000 murders before she turns 18.  _ Teenagers are exposed to 14,000 sexual references and innuendos per year on TV. Only 150 of these deal with responsibility, abstinence or birth control.  _ African Americans make up only 5 percent of the casts in children's programming, while, in fact, they make up 12.1 percent of the U.S. population.  _ Even though 10 percent of the U.S. population is Latino, a child sees only one Latino character every other weekend out of the 123 characters on Saturday morning TV. (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (--Mexico, Central America, South America: PAUL FINCH iAngeles, 310-996-0075; fax, 310-996-0089. Includes a 300-word sidebar. By KATHY SENA From Los Angeles Times c. 1996 Kathy Sena (Distributed by New York Times Syndicate.)  When the owner decided to sell the Los Feliz, Calif., home where Eva Bizar and her husband were living, Bizar was prepared to be helpful. She polished the floors, kept the kitchen spotless and agreed to stay away from home for an afternoon during an open house arranged by the real estate agent.  What she wasn't prepared for was the two men who blended in with the crowd during the open house and made off with her jewelry _ including a much-loved diamond anniversary band.  ``I couldn't believe some people would go to an open house with the express purpose of stealing things,'' Bizar says.  But that's exactly what about 5 percent of the people who attend open houses are doing, says Gregg McMullin, a police officer for the Manhattan Beach, Calif., Police Department's crime prevention unit.  ``You get a real cross-section of people at an open house,'' he adds. ``You see buyers and you see burglars pretending to be buyers.''  It's tough for a real estate agent to keep a constant watch on everyone who wanders through an open house, McMullin says, ``so you need to control things as much as possible.''  Bizar thought she was doing just that when, in preparation for the open house, she placed her frequently worn jewelry in a box and tucked it in the back of a drawer in the bathroom.  ``I thought I was being careful,'' she says. ``I didn't leave the jewelry out on a dresser, waiting to be stolen.'' But the thieves were in and out of the house in minutes. COVERING YOUR ASSETS  Unfortunately, Bizar isn't alone. Homeowners all across the country have reported having expensive family heirlooms, collectibles _ even prescription drugs _ stolen during an open house. And many of these items aren't covered under a standard homeowner's policy, says Robert Ray, a registered representative with Prudential Property and Casualty Insurance Co. in Redondo Beach, Calif.  ``Items stolen during an open house would be (covered under the category) `mysterious disappearance,''' he explains, noting that items valued at more than $500 should probably be scheduled separately on a homeowner's policy.  But even if these items are insured, he says, you should still take precautions.  ``I tell people to put valuables in their safe deposit box during an open house,'' Ray says, ``Why take the risk?'' PROTECTING YOUR VALUABLES  But what if your autographed Mickey Mantle baseball won't fit in your safe deposit box? There are lots of safe places to hide your valuables, according to experts.  The main objective is to get your valuables out of reach, not just out of sight. Try the attic, the trunk of your car or the neighbor's house.  ``You might as well pack up everything of value, because you're going to be moving anyway,'' says Susan Stearns, a realtor associate with Prudential Jon Douglas West Valley in Los Angeles.  ``I always ask the neighbors to be on the lookout for suspicious activity when I'm having an open house,'' she adds.  Also, make sure everyone who enters the home signs the guest register, is acknowledged by the agent, and _ if possible _ is accompanied by the agent as they tour your home.  Having two agents working an open house together can be a great security measure for both the seller and for the agents themselves.  ``With team open houses you can have one agent at the door greeting people and the other agent showing prospective buyers through the house,'' says Dorothy LaRose, an associate broker with Shorewood Realtors in Manhattan Beach.  With just one agent, ``you can't cover a large or a two-story house,'' she adds. FOILING FUTURE BURGLARS  Keep in mind, as you're doing your pre-open-house security check, that a potential burglar may stop by the open house to check out your home for a future break-in.  ``You want to make your home looks lived-in, whether or not you're living there at the time,'' McMullin says. For instance, don't leave a stack of unopened mail or newspapers in plain view.  It's also wise to mark large valuables, such as televisions or stereos, with your driver's license number for easier retrieval in the event of a burglary, he adds. Contact your local police department to borrow an engraver and then inscribe the number into the metal.  ``And don't forget to write down the serial numbers of your television, VCR and stereo,'' McMullin says.  If you have an extra car parked at home during an open house, don't leave the car keys on your dresser or on a hook in the kitchen. That's an invitation to a car thief, McMullin says.  ``Put the keys somewhere such as a suit jacket pocket,'' he suggests.  Credit card receipts left on the dresser are an open invitation to a thief who would love to have your Visa number. Toss them out, and while you're at it, take the trash out too.  ``Thieves like to look in your trash for credit card information and for information about your whereabouts,'' McMullin says.  Your family calendar _ the one in the kitchen noting Jimmy's soccer games and Susie's school play _ can also be a source of valuable information to a burglar. During your open house, don't let the world know you're heading to Grandma's for a week next month.  Also, ask you agent in advance to avoid giving out your schedule inadvertently, Stearns advises.  ``Even if you're at work all day, you want your agent telling people, `Yes, they're home all the time.''' WHO'S RESPONSIBLE?  What if, despite all your precautions, you do have a theft during an open house? Is your real estate agent responsible?  ``(Agents) really don't have any liability unless they are negligent,'' says Gov Hutchinson, assistant general council to the California Association of Realtors in Los Angeles.  Hutchinson explains that, when it comes to safeguarding a seller's property, agents are held to a ``general negligence standard,'' and to be liable, ``the agent would have to do something such as leaving the house unlocked or letting in a known criminal.''  But even though the final responsibility generally rests with the homeowner, agents should explain to owners the element of risk inherent in holding an open house. And sellers should discuss with agents _ in advance and in detail _ how they want their property handled. A CALCULATED RISK  In the end, no matter how careful you are, you're still taking a risk when you open your home to the public. And while holding open houses may increase an agent's overall exposure and bring in potential new business, the average owner's chances of selling a home as a result of an open house are only about 1 percent, estimates Marty Rodriguez, co-owner of Century 21 in Glendora, Calif.  It's important for the owner to weigh both the risks and the benefits of flinging open the doors to the public. Just ask Eva Bizar.  ``People need to be aware of the potential problems when they have an open house,'' she says. ``I never thought this would happen to us.'' SIDEBAR: OPEN HOUSE SECURITY CHECKLIST  While it's easy to remember to remove Grandma's ivory pendant from the jewelry box before an open house, here are some other not-so-obvious things you might want to remove from your home:  _ Small electronic items. Remove portable CD players, radios, cameras, CDs and cassette tapes.  ``Anything that's concealable is a possible target for theft,'' according to Gregg McMullin, a police officer for the Manhattan Beach, Calif., Police Department's crime prevention unit.  Cordless and cellular phones are also popular items.  _ Guns and ammunition. While deterring theft is an obvious reason to remove these items, protecting curious children from possible disaster is even more important. Putting a gun on a high closet shelf isn't enough. Get it out of the house.  _ Small items that might be fascinating to a child.  ``I have a sterling silver bird from Tiffany's,'' says Delia Delgado, a real estate associate with Re/Max South County in Newport Beach, Calif. ``I sure wouldn't want a child to walk off with it, thinking it was a neat toy.''  While young children might not steal something intentionally, it would be easy for a youngster to go home with a bird in the hand.  _ Valuable artwork, figurines and collectibles.  _ Silverware and crystal. Pack it before moving and store it before the open house. An added bonus: Your cupboards will appear more spacious.  _ Cash, credit cards, watches and keys. This may seem obvious, but how many of us have a change jar on the dresser, or emergency cash in the sock drawer? And who doesn't routinely leave a watch or keys on the dresser or nightstand?  _ Garage door openers. ``If you leave your garage-door opener in an unlocked car or on the kitchen counter, someone could walk off with it and break in later,'' Delgado says.  (Kathy Sena is a Los Angeles-based freelance writer.)  -------------------------------------------------------------  (To publish this ``separate buy'' article it must be purchased -- the rate is not prohibitive -- from one of these New York Times Syndicate sales representatives:  (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Tim Roth with Tupac Shakur in a scene from ``Gridlock'd.'' By IAN SPELLING (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  Don't call Tim Roth ``the king of the independent film.''  ``That's a journalist's term,'' the British actor says. ``It's not what I call myself.''  Still, Roth's credentials are impressive, including intense performances in offbeat films ranging from ``The Cook, the Thief, His Wife and Her Lover'' (1989) and ``Vincent and Theo'' (1990) to ``Reservoir Dogs'' (1992) and ``Pulp Fiction'' (1994).  All were independent, non-studio productions. In fact, Roth's resume includes only two studio releases, ``Rob Roy'' (1995) and the upcoming ``Hoodlums.''  ``It's not snobbery,'' the 35-year-old Roth says by phone from the Los Angeles home he shares with his wife and two kids. ``It's that the scripts I liked were coming out of the independent world.  ``It's still that way,'' he adds.  Roth's latest film is ``Gridlock'd,'' a comedy about three performance artists, Stretch (Roth), Spoon (the late Tupac Shakur) and Cookie (Thandie Newton) that opens Jan. 29. When Cookie overdoses and nearly dies, Stretch and Spoon resolve to kick their heroin habits.  It isn't that easy, though. Before they know it, the two are on the run, suspected of murder.  ``It's not the usual subject matter for a comedy, which I liked,'' Roth says. ``You have a Laurel-and-Hardy thing going with me and Tupac.  ``Tupac was the straight man to my character, who's a little more nutty than his. I bounced off him,'' he adds. ``The chance to play that kind of comedy appealed to me.  ``It's a film I'd see,'' he says, ``so it's the kind of film I like to do.''  Shakur was shot in September 1996, soon after completing the picture, and died soon afterward. But the gangsta rapper whose death drew denunciations of his music and his lifestyle was not the Shakur that Roth knew.  The rapper actually asked director Vondie Curtis-Hall not to screen his films or music videos for Roth. He wanted Roth to meet him first.  ``The idea, I suppose, was that he thought he was a very different animal from how he portrayed himself in the media and his other work,'' Roth says. ``I met him and he seemed charming and enthusiastic. He really wanted the role.''  Shakur won the part and, while on set, he and Roth frequently discussed the likelihood of Shakur dying young. After all, Shakur had survived a previous shooting.  ``We talked about how you get out of a situation you've put yourself in,'' Roth says. ``Tupac said that was difficult, because if you walk away too fast, they'll want you.  ``If you try to slide away and move into different areas, they'll still want you,'' he says. ``It seemed to him to be a Catch-22.''  When Roth heard that Shakur had been gunned down in Las Vegas, he was shocked.  ``Conversations are one thing, but the reality is another,'' he says. ``Tupac always felt it was going to happen.  ``But when he was shot, there was that thought, `He pulled through before, he'll pull through again,''' the actor says. ``It didn't happen this time.''  Roth says he's glad that ``Gridlock'd'' is being released. He hopes it will help the moviegoing public judge Shakur for themselves.  ``Tupac did some nice acting,'' Roth says. ``He was natural and would have gotten even better.''  Like Shakur, Roth found his way into acting indirectly. Growing up in London, he hoped to be a sculptor and painter.  But a role in a high-school play hooked him, and acting became his passion. Stage roles followed, then parts in British TV programs and films.  Roth's big break came when he starred in the British TV movie ``Meantime'' (1983) for acclaimed director Mike Leigh. He has worked steadily since, doing films with such admired directors as Robert Altman, Peter Greenaway, Stephen Frears and Quentin Tarantino.  ``Those guys are completely different in the ways they work, the ways they communicate with actors,'' Roth says. ``Most of them are completely driven and obsessive, to the point where they'll bring the film down if they can't do it their way.  ``Which I like,'' he adds with a chuckle. ``Early on, I worked with Mike, and that stays with you, working within his (improvisational) process.  ``Altman makes you feel you have absolute freedom,'' he says. ``Quentin, you just ride on his insanity and trust him.  ``I'd work with any of them again at the drop of a hat,'' he concludes.  Despite occasional surprises such as ``Pulp Fiction,'' it's rare for independent features to capture the collective imagination of mainstream moviegoers. Thus, budgets are low and creative choices can be constricted.  ``There are huge risks and it's amazing that people actually front them,'' Roth says. ``It's so hit-and-miss. In the end, the risks are more worth taking than not.''  Despite his preference for the cutting edge of independent films, Roth clearly enjoyed his role in ``Rob Roy'' as the flamboyant English nemesis of Scottish hero Rob Roy MacGregor (Liam Neeson).  The film was Roth's first with a hefty budget, and let him get in some agile fencing. It also garnered him an Oscar nomination for Best Supporting Actor.  ``Rob Roy'' also led to a number of offers for mainstream roles, usually as diabolical villains in action movies.  ``I was asked to do a `Die Hard' movie,'' Roth says, laughing an evil laugh. ``I like the `Lethal Weapon' series, actually. Those things are fun, and I might do one some day.''  Currently he can be seen in Woody Allen's musical, ``Everyone Says I Love You,''casts Roth as a newly freed criminal who romances wholesome Drew Barrymore.  Every actor on Earth seems to pine to work with Allen, and Roth was no exception.  ``It was a strange experience,'' he says of ``Everyone,'' which opens nationwide throughout February. ``Woody I really enjoyed, but I was there an awfully long time for my two big scenes.  ``In all, I shot for seven days but was there for six weeks, because Woody pretty much makes up the schedule as he goes,'' he says. ``I did always want to work with him, and I've done it.  ``There you go,'' he adds with a laugh. ``I'd love to do it again.''  In the meantime, however, Roth continues to work at a breakneck pace in the quirky world of independents. His upcoming films include ``Gasoline Alley,'' a drama about two brothers, and ``Liar,'' a psychological thriller.  Then there's the mainstream ``Hoodlums,'' a 1930s gangster saga with Laurence Fishburne and Vanessa Williams in which Roth plays Dutch Schultz.  Next up may be ``The War Zone,'' based on the Alexander Stuart novel about an abused 14-year-old boy. Roth plans to make his directorial debut with the film, and is currently struggling with paperwork in anticipation of filming in the autumn.  ``I have no idea what I'll bring to the table as a director,'' he says. ``What I'm realizing already, from meetings with lawyers and financiers, is that the difference between being an actor and a director is that you have to be a grown-up to direct.  ``People ask the director grown-up questions,'' he says. ``Actors can be children and, in fact, they're expected to be children.''  Even if ``War Zone'' is a hit, Roth plans to keep acting at his current breakneck pace. For all his protests, it is clearly his true love.  ``My work is a huge part of who I am, I suppose,'' he says. ``I'm deeply unhappy when I'm not working.  ``I bitch and moan if I've been working nonstop and I'm exhausted,'' he adds. ``But if I stop for more than a couple of weeks, I'm dead.'' c.1997 Ian Spelling  (Ian Spelling is a New York-based free-lance writer.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) ------------------------------------------------- </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19970113.0217 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-JARED-LETO-IN-`PREFONTAINE'-PAGEUP-ART-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> JARED LETO'S FAST TRACK TO STARDOM (This ``separate buy'' article is from this week's PageUp Lifestyle and Entertainment Package.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Jared Leto running in a scene from ``Prefontaine.'' By CINDY PEARLMAN World rights except Chicago (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  This might be the only Jared Leto interview you'll ever read.  ``After doing an interview I feel like a guy who got drunk the night before, did something embarrassing and then has to go to school or work the next morning and live it down,'' the press-shy actor says.  Sitting on the patio of his Los Angeles home in shorts, a T-shirt and bare feet, his dog romping nearby, Leto shrugs.  ``I'm keeping my mouth shut,'' he says. ``I gotta keep a little mystery.''  If you're over 25 or male, you may be wondering who Jared Leto isacting like such a star commodity. But if you're female and under 25, you probably don't need to ask. Since making a splash in the 1994 ABC series ``My So-Called Life,'' the 25-year-old Leto has had the heart of young female America on a string.  At malls, girls scream when they glimpse him. Fan letters pour in by the bagful. One national magazine dubbed him a ``James Dean for the 1990s,'' though he's less brooding than Dean. He's attitude combined with inner turmoil _ not to mention the bluest eyes around.  And now Leto's film career is off and running _ literally _ in ``Prefontaine,'' opening Jan. 24. He plays Olympic runner Steve Prefontaine, who died tragically in a car crash at age 24.  Next month he will be seen as a serial-killer suspect opposite Dennis Quaid and Danny Glover in ``Going West,'' and later this year comes ``Last of the High Kings,'' with Gabriel Byrne.  Young fans will no doubt line up for miles. But don't mention the words ``teen idol.''  ``I hate when I see these young guys go, `Oh, I'm a real actor and not a teen idol,''' Leto gripes. Nevertheless, he adds, ``I'm controlling things so I don't become a teen idol. I don't do photo shoots, I don't do mall appearances. That's disgusting stuff. I'm not creating this following.''  Leto was a self-professed ``weird kid,'' growing up with his older brother Shannon and their single mother in a nomadic lifestyle that included stops in Louisiana, Colorado, Virginia, Wyoming and Haiti.  ``There was no reason,'' Leto says. ``It was a hippie kind of thing.''  Growing up, Leto had no particular plans for his future, which sets him apart sharply from the character he plays. Steve ``Pre'' Prefontaine was noted primarily for his iron determination.  Born in Oregon, Prefontaine had everything going against him as a runner _ one of his legs was shorter than the other, some said he was too small and others that he wasn't fast enough.  ``These people forgot something,'' Prefontaine said. ``I have to win.''  Prefontaine became world-famous at the 1972 Munich Olympics, and held every American distance record from 2,000 meters to 10,000 meters until his death.  Even making the film involved a race of sorts: Another film about the athlete, called ``Pre,'' produced by Tom Cruise and starring newcomer Billy Crudup, will open this fall. Unlike ``Prefontaine,'' it did not have the consent or assistance of the runner's family.  Director Steve James, creator of ``Hoop Dreams'' (1994), picked Leto for the role after producer Irby Smith spotted him on MTV reruns of ``So-Called Life.''  ``I guess it didn't hurt that I'm a dead ringer for Steve Prefontaine,'' Leto says. ``It's all in the eyes. He had very intense eyes, and people have said the same thing about me.  ``Pre's real sister came to the set one day,'' he adds, ``and she took one look at me and burst into tears. I was real sorry that she was crying, but inside I couldn't help being happy because I thought, `We're really getting this thing right.'''  Getting it right wasn't easy. Leto isn't a runner, to begin with. And on his third day of training _ with coach Pat Tyson, a former teammate of Prefontaine's at the University of Oregon _ he blew his knee out.  ``I wanted to cry,'' he admits. ``I just couldn't believe this happened. We had to start filming two months later.''  Leto went into intense rehabilitation therapy.  ``I had what they call electrostimulation, where they give you little shock treatments to build up the tissue,'' he says. ``The rest was a lot of Advil. The medic at our training center always had an ice bucket for me to literally jump into after doing a lap.''  For the film, Leto had to run only in short bursts of 200 meters.  ``Still, it was exhausting, because we had days when I would be running on and off for 12 hours,'' he says. ``I had to keep reminding myself that single mothers on welfare had it a lot tougher, and I should just shut up because all I was doing was making a movie.''  That he is making movies is in its own way quite remarkable. Leto began acting only three years ago, when he moved to Los Angeles. Previously he had never acted, not even in a school play.  Six months later, he was cast in ``My So-Called Life,'' and his career took off.  ``When I got it, I had no idea it was going to be good,'' he admits. ``I thought it was some dumb teen show. Then I saw the pilot and I was blown away. It hooked me emotionally.''  When ``My So-Called Life'' was canceled after one season because of star Claire Danes's desire to pursue a movie career, Leto declined various offers to star in teen movies.  ``I'm not that much an idiot,'' he says. ``I'm interested in acting, not posturing.  ``For me, I want to be excited when I read a script,'' Leto says. ``I have to go, `Wow!' It's like when I read `Prefontaine.' I said, `I gotta do it.'''  Nevertheless, he doesn't plan to see the film.  ``I'd sit there and go, `Oh God, they picked that take? I want to kill myself,''' he says. ``When I see myself on screen, I just see everything that I did wrong.'''  As to the future, Leto is set on a film career, but is troubled by a sense of his own mortality. He believes that, like Steve Prefontaine, he may be destined to die young.  ``A palmreader once told me that I'm going to have a short life,'' he says matter-of-factly. ``I also get letters from some wacko who tells me that I'm going to die before a certain age. It's just this creepy thing that I live with.  ``I guess that's why I want to be good in everything I do and not waste my chances,'' he says. ``I want to make every moment and every movie count.''  This focus extends even to his personal life. He doesn't have time for dating, Leto says, or even many friends.  ``Honestly, I don't think I've ever had a date,'' he says. ``I'm not the dating type. I didn't have a date in all of high school.''  The actor likes to think of himself as a loner.  ``I like that word, `loner.' It sounds sort of romantic,'' he says. ``I've got friends. I just don't know who they are.''  Instead, his time is spent working _ and, believe it or not, running, a habit he picked up during ``Prefontaine.''  ``It's always just me running around by myself,'' Leto says, ``through the empty streets and up the Hollywood hills like the young loner I am.  ``See, girls?'' he says with a laugh. ``I'm just a cliche.'' c.1997 Cindy Pearlman  (Cindy Pearlman is a Chicago-based free-lance writer.) (To publish this ``separate buy'' article it must be purchased -- the rate is not prohibitive -- from one of these New York Times Syndicate sales representatives: (--U.S., Canada and the Pacific: CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City, 816-822-8448 or fax her at 816-822-1444. (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (--Mexico, Central America, South America: PAUL FINCH in Los Angeles, 310-996-0075; fax, 310-996-0089. Includes a 50-word sidebar. By LAURA VITALE c.1996 Diversion (Distributed by New York Times Syndicate.)  Last year a friend and I, in an effort to fill a few holes in our CD collections, each considered joining one of those mail-order music clubs _ the ones advertised in magazine inserts that boast hundreds of selections and amazing offers such as, in our case, ``10 CDs for the Price of One with Nothing More to Buy, Ever!''  Only problem was, neither of us was able to find 10 selections we liked in order to take advantage of the introductory deal. So she, an alternative rock fan, and I, one who prefers '60s music, decided to join a club together under a single membership. We each received five CDs that we wanted, and we split the cost.  Mail-order music clubs work much like book clubs. They offer special deals to get you to join; then they usually require you to buy a certain number of titles (most say just one) within a specified period of time.  The major clubs _ Columbia House, BMG Music Service and CD Headquarters _ advertise in national magazines (Columbia House also solicits by mail) and send out monthly catalogs to their members. Both Columbia and BMG offer cassettes as well as CDs. The clubs also mail a featured selection every month unless you return a response card telling them not to. DO THESE CLUBS SAVE YOU MONEY?  It depends. No one can go wrong by taking advantage of one of the amazing introductory offers. During certain offers you can get as many as 12 CDs for the price of one (though you will also be billed about $2 shipping and handling for each CD).  Follow-up purchases, though, are a different matter. CDs bought in stores in most parts of the country cost $11.99 to $16.99; in some instances, you might be able to find them for as little as $8.99. After you fulfill the terms of your initial offer, future club CDs cost $12.98 to $16.98, plus those shipping and handling charges. No savings there.  But club members are rewarded with a variety of perks. For instance, CD Headquarters (CDHQ) is offering its members such deals as ``buy one disc get two free,'' and BMG Music Service is telling first-time subscribers to buy a selection at the regular club price and all additional selections are just $5.99 per disc. And both BMG and Columbia reward members with four free CDs for getting a friend to join.  So if you buy carefully and take advantage of the perks, you can come out ahead. SCOPE OUT THE BEST SELECTION  Anyone thinking about joining should investigate just what each club has to offer before signing up. The mail-order companies cater to a range of musical tastes, with such categories as hard rock, alternative, R&AMP;B, country, jazz and classical.  Columbia probably has the widest selection, including Latin and gospel. CDHQ specializes in rock and alternative choices, but lacks a classical category. BMG Music Service has a selection similar to but slightly less extensive than Columbia's.  Of course, a club's total repertoire is not represented by one given ad. And just because you don't see a certain release in any of a club's ads doesn't mean it isn't in stock. Call the company and inquire.  Remember, even when making your introductory selection, you do not have to choose solely from the ad that contains the introductory order form. Rock enthusiasts will find plenty to choose from in the ads in Rolling Stone and Spin, but fans of classical music might have a more difficult time; the clubs, intent on mass sales, list few if any selections from this category.  The best bet is to call Columbia and BMG directly and request their classical catalogs. All the clubs have catalogs for each of their categories and will mail one or several on request, whether you are a member or not.  For those with access to the Internet, Columbia and BMG have their entire catalogs online. The CD Club Web Server, a Web site that includes information on current offers and sales, also contains the catalogs of all three clubs, so you can compare the clubs' selections for a given artist at a glance.  In general, don't expect a thorough, up-to-date selection from any of these companies. Because they pay musicians lower royalties than do traditional distributors, some performers prohibit the sale of their releases by the clubs.  And music lovers whose tastes are far from mainstream are better off staying away altogether: If you're an aficionado of eccentric lounge music, for instance, you probably won't find anything that will shake your maracas.  When you join a club, you're asked to indicate your favorite music category, and this determines which catalog and featured selection you'll receive each month from the club. But you are not locked into that category; again, call your club to request other catalogs or to inquire about a given release. SHOP AROUND  The three big clubs frequently advertise different introductory offers in different publications. For instance, currently Columbia House is offering 11 CDs for the price of one. BMG also had ads for both 11 and 12 CDs. Thumb through a number of music and entertainment publications until you find the best deal.  And don't forget to read the fine print that appears on the order form. The clubs will send you several ``free'' CDs initially, then send the remaining few after you've made a purchase. If you respond to Columbia's ``11 CDs for the Price of One'' offer, you may pay the price for only one CD (plus shipping and handling charges) and a $1.49 ``enrollment charge''; then you agree to buy six more selections at regular club prices.  Granted, Columbia gives you three years in which to do this, but you should fulfill the bargain as quickly as possible, otherwise you'll be flooded with statements, invoices and other unwanted surprises. Try to avoid any offers that lock you into buying more than one CD after you join. MAKE SURE YOU'RE GETTING WHAT YOU WANT  Before ordering, it doesn't hurt to do a little research in your local record store. Many releases have been remastered in recent years; if a CD you want fits into this category, find out if the advertised selection is, indeed, the latest, remastered version.  If the club's customer service representative doesn't know, you might choose to order the CD anyway. If it isn't the version you want, call the club and explain the situation. You can send back the unopened CD (be sure to request a postage-paid address label). READ THE MAILINGS  While you're grooving to the recordings you received from your introductory offer, read the little welcome booklet that accompanied them, carefully. It explains how to fill out the club's notification card. This is a little piece of paper the club sends each month: Ignore it and your life will become a living hell.  That's because each club automatically sends you its featured selection _ the club's choice in the musical area of interest you specified on your introductory offer form _ each month, unless you indicate that you'd prefer a substitute selection or none at all.  Take note that, even if you diligently inspect all club mailings and shipments, there's a good chance mistakes will still occur.  And don't expect to see the CDs you ordered right away: the clubs ship the CDs via fourth-class mail, which means your items can take about a month to reach you, maybe longer. THE BOTTOM LINE  Once you've taken advantage of a club's introductory offer, should you stay for the long haul?  Obviously, those of you who are just beginning to build your CD collections will benefit from the sales, bonuses and freebies down the road. But if your collection is already comprehensive, it will be difficult to find titles you don't already own.  For those who live outside major metropolitan areas and don't have access to music megastores, the clubs can be a great resource. But no matter where you live, if a club's inventory isn't to your liking, there's no use sticking around.  Having met the terms of BMG's agreement, my friend and I quit by phone and in writing. (Anyone whose conscience is bothered by our move should note that no sooner had I canceled than I received a mailing from the club promoting a new introductory offer.)  I quit primarily because the club's selection didn't appeal to me. Also, I didn't want to be bothered with all the invoices and response cards.  So is it worth a shot? Sure. As I said, you can always quit. -0- SIDEBAR: MUSIC BY THE NUMBERS  _ BMG Music Service 317-692-9200; 317-542-6244 (fax); 70007.1532compuserve.com (e-mail); http://www.bmgmusicservice.com (Web site)  _ Columbia House 800-457-0500; http://www.columbiahouse.com  _ CD Headquarters 800-242-7096  _ The CD Club Web Server http://www.hirschhorn.com/cdclubs/  (Laura Vitale is copy chief at Diversion magazine.)  ----------------------------------------------  (To publish this ``separate buy'' article it must be purchased -- the rate is not prohibitive -- from one of these New York Times Syndicate sales representatives:  (--Europe and Asia: KARL HORWITZ in Paris at 47-42-17-11; fax, 47-42-80-44 or 47-42-18-81; telex, 282-942. (--U.S., Canada and the Pacific: CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City, 816-822-8448 or fax her at 816-822-1444. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19970113.0219 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-JANE-CURTIN-IN-`3RD-ROCK'-PAGEUP-ART-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> JANE CURTIN, `ROCK' STAR (This ``separate buy'' article is from this week's PageUp Lifestyle and Entertainment Package.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Jane Curtin in a scene from ``3rd Rock from the Sun.'' By PETER MARKS c.1996 The New York Times (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  Jane Curtin was cleaning her kitchen in Connecticut one frigid January evening when the telephone rang.  It was her old friend, the comedy writer Bonnie Turner. The call was not spur of the moment. This was a television emergency. Would Curtin, she wanted to know, consider another go at a sitcom?  Turner, it seemed, was in a bind. She and her husband, Terry, writing partners with whom Curtin had worked on ``Saturday Night Live'' and the 1993 film ``The Coneheads,'' were developing a new comedy about a group of eccentric aliens who come to Earth and take human form.  The writers urgently required a straight woman with a bit of an edge to play the aliens' thoroughly human foil, and Curtin sprang to mind.  ``They'd shot the pilot, and the character that they created for the love interest didn't quite work,'' Curtin says. ``They had made aracter too sweet, and so there was no tension.''  Although Curtin said she was committed to another project and was not available, Turner was not put off. She told Curtin she could have the role on her own terms.  ``She said, `You only have to do as many shows as you want,''' the actress recalls. ``I said, `OK, seven.' And she said, `Seven it is.'''  As effortlessly as that, Curtin began her re-entry into a universe she had already conquered in five seasons on ``Kate and Allie,'' the successful 1980s sitcom in which she was a co-star with Susan Saint James.  The new show, ``3rd Rock From the Sun,'' which also stars John Lithgow _ who won a 1996 Emmy for his sitcom debut in this role _ would prove to be another hit for Curtin, an actress who seems unable to miss on series television.  One of the original Not Ready for Prime Time Players on ``Saturday Night Live,'' on which she appeared for five seasons ending in 1980, Curtin was already television-comedy royalty when she signed on to ``Kate and Allie'' in 1984.  ``3rd Rock,'' which had its premiere on NBC in January 1996, completes the actress' comedic hat trick. In The New York Times, the television critic John J. O'Connor called the show ``brilliantly idiotic, a daffy pastiche of farce, burlesque and satire.''  Of all the original ``Saturday Night Live'' cast members, Curtin has had by far the most successful television career. Others who joined ``Saturday Night'' in subsequent years, such as Bill Murray and Eddie Murphy, went on to film stardom or graduated to standout roles in prime time, as did Julia-Louis Dreyfus and Phil Hartman.  But no other ``Saturday Night'' alum can match Curtin for such sustained achievement on acclaimed shows.  ``There's really no formula to this _ you just get lucky,'' Curtin says by telephone from her dressing room in a television studio in Los Angeles, where ``3rd Rock From the Sun'' is taped.  ``I was always hoping for a life in television, but I didn't know what to imagine after `Saturday Night Live,''' she says. ``I had done a movie while I was on `Saturday Night,' and I had done theater before that, but I really liked television.  ``I liked the immediacy of it, the fact that you didn't have to repeat yourself every week and that it was fast,'' she says. ``Ideally, a sitcom is like a little play each week, and I liked that instant gratification.''  On ``3rd Rock From the Sun,'' Curtin plays Dr. Mary Albright, a professor at a Midwestern college who shares an office with Dick Solomon, the human identity that Lithgow's alien character has assumed.  The show revels in its own silliness. Much of the sitcom's humor derives from the absurdities of everyday life as revealed by a family of aliens who are entirely unfamiliar with the mores of expected behavior.  Albright may be a scholar, but she's a little thick when it comes to the bizarre goings-on in the Solomon household, populated by Lithgow and his compadres from outer space, played by Kristen Johnson, French Stewart and Joseph Gordon-Levitt.  It may be that she is blinded by her attraction to Lithgow's character. In any event, she is the oblivious/suspicious human bystander, a role with a great tradition in supernatural sitcoms, from Gladys Kravitz, the nosy neighbor in ``Bewitched,'' to Dr. Bellows, the snooty superior officer in ``I Dream of Jeanie.''  Curtin laughs easily about her exploits on ``3rd Rock.'' She takes the show no more seriously than do viewers.  ``It's actually about nothing,'' the actress says, a characterization that in the age of ``Seinfeld'' is a compliment. ``It's just about the way human beings behave.  ``I loved the character, but I also loved the sense of humor on the show,'' she says. ``I had known John in New York, and I'm very comfortable with him. It made me laugh when John and I were working together _ there just would be puddles on the floor.''  Eventually, Curtin agreed to extend her limited commitment to the show. In July, she and her family, diehard Easterners, moved to Los Angeles.  ``We decided to give it a go,'' she says, adding that her daughter is attending high school there.  A semblance of normalcy amid the insanity of weekly television is important to Curtin. One of the things she loved most about ``Kate and Allie,'' she says, was that she was able to walk from her apartment in Manhattan to the studio in the Ed Sullivan Theater, where the show was taped and where David Letterman now performs nightly.  Curtin is not a flashy performer. Unlike many who made their names on ``Saturday Night Live,'' she had no quintessential Jane Curtin character, no Church Lady or Buckwheat or Samurai Dry Cleaner. Curtin made an art of fitting in wherever she was needed, a talent that has served her well through the years.  With continued television success has come a comfort level with her abilities and a confidence that there will always be a place for her.  ``It's gotten to the point where if it wasn't going to be fun, I didn't want to do it,'' she says of the job offers she has turned down. ``If it wasn't going to be right or you aren't interested in the people involved, you should be comfortable enough not to be intimidated into doing it.''  At the moment, she is plenty comfortable. The 20-minute commute from home to studio agrees with her, as does life in California, to her surprise.  ``I keep trying to tell friends of mine in New York who are theater people or movie people what it's like to do a sitcom,'' she says. ``I paint a very rosy picture. And I don't think they believe me.''  (Peter Marks reports on entertainment for The New York Times.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) ------------------------------------------------- </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19970113.0220 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-SINGER-ANITA-O'DAY-PAGEUP-ART-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> THE ACCIDENTAL LEGEND OF ANITA O'DAY (This ``separate buy'' article is from this week's PageUp Lifestyle and Entertainment Package.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of singer Anita O'Day brandishing a microphone. By LEONCE GAITER (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  Anita O'Day never begged a lover to come back to her.  ``I Told You I Love You, Now Get Out'' was a signature song. She sings her joys and pains with a playful, sometimes wistful resignation. Her style knows little of tragedy or high drama. Her songs, and her life, reek of irony and steel, not tears and regrets.  This January, O'Day received the Jazz Masters Award from the National Endowment for the Arts, the highest award offered to a jazz artist. Finally, this often-overlooked, too-often-underrated jazz vocal pioneer is receiving her due.  In addition, with the recent CD release of ``Jazz Masters 49: Anita O'Day'' (Verve, 1996), a compilation of 1950s and 196Os recordings, listeners can finally experience the breadth and depth of this master of the jazz vocal.  And this is no valedictory _ O'Day is still singing. She has a new album in the works, and she still performs at such venues as Los Angeles' Jazz Bakery and New York's Tavern on the Green.  Standing in the lobby of her Hollywood hotel, O'Day seems anything but a jazz diva, smoking an unfiltered Camel and entertaining the guard on duty. In town from her modest California desert digs to sing at a benefit at the Beverly Hills Hotel, she seems self-contained and at peace with herself.  At 75, O'Day still exudes the rugged energy of her early recordings. But hers is not a story of success achieved through canny career planning and driving ambition. Indeed, she is almost a fluke, an accidental legend.  While some singers wlding careers and networking, O'Day was singing, night after night, for little reward beyond the opportunity to sing again the next night. Her tunnel vision was so intense that when she broke up with her longtime companion, this senior citizen had to learn how to keep a checkbook and how a credit card works. She didn't even know to whom she should pay her rent.  All along, she'd focused on one thing, and one thing alone _ her songs.  ``When I met her in the 1970s,'' says her current manager, Alan Eichler, ``she was working little clubs around town for 50 bucks a night or 100 bucks a night, whatever she could get.  ``At Dante's in the Valley, she would work for $100, and the owner would make her sit there until he was closing to pay her,'' she says. ``She would finish her set and had to sit there for two hours waiting to get paid.  ``She has no ego,'' he adds. ``She doesn't think of herself as a star.''  ``I don't play that,'' O'Day says dismissively. ``When I listen to a whole bunch of singers out there, I'll say `Yeah, I sound pretty good,' but I don't linger on it. When it's showtime, it's showtime.''  Knowing nothing of the ``business,'' O'Day never made any effort to build a marketable act or even to cultivate the connections that might have led to a dependable recording career. She never modified her sometimes-eccentric behavior to attract record executives, mollify producers or win friends.  Her one incredibly fertile recording period occurred in the 1950s when jazz impresario Norman Granz signed her to his Verve label. There she recorded her masterpieces ``Anita Sings the Most,'' with Oscar Peterson on piano, and ``Pick Yourself Up,'' with jazz combo and big band, along with the excellent ``Anita.''  The singer remembers the Peterson sessions vividly.  ``I just thought he was gonna play piano for me,'' she recalls. ``Instead, he played Oscar Peterson. I really had to get out there, and when the horses were coming around the back side and going into the stretch, I said to myself, `This son of a bitch is playing so many notes, where am I?'  ``I had to scramble to try to get back on top, but he always nosed in ahead of me,'' she says. ``It was great fun. I lost twelve races. But sometimes it's fun to lose.''  It can also be fruitful. That album is arguably her greatest, feeding off the frisson between Peterson's effusive, bluesy pianism and O'Day's off-kilter, white-girl phrasings. It's a masterpiece of full-out, pedal-to-the-metal jazz.  O'Day came by her jazz mastery the hard way, having been singing professionally since 1934, when she was 14.  According to her autobiography, ``High Times, Hard Times'' (Limelight Editions, 1989), that was when she told her mother that she was ``too dumb'' for school and that she was off to be a singer in the Walkathons, the grueling Depression-era dance marathons depicted so vividly in the 1969 film, ``They Shoot Horses, Don't They?''  Already she was displaying the lack of forethought and planning that would become her trademark, especially since her voice was an unconventional one, far from the style of fellow jazz masters Mildred Bailey, Billie Holiday, Ella Fitzgerald and Sarah Vaughan.  O'Day had always lacked a ``pretty'' voice. She has no ``tone,'' which she blames on her lack of a uvula, the little ``tongue'' that hangs down in the back of the throat. A careless dentist had removed hers, along with her tonsils, when she was 7.  As a result, O'Day was forced to emphasize faster tempos and 16th notes.  ``Instead of singing, `Laaaaa,' I'd sing `La-la-la-la-la-la,' to keep it moving,'' she says.  To this day, O'Day insists that she doesn't really have a voice.  ``But I'm not a singer,'' she says. ``I'm a song stylist.''  Singer or not, by 1941 she had caught on with Gene Krupa's band.  ``After I joined Krupa, I was just out there every day to learn something from every job,'' she says. ``I was always happy, no matter what the working conditions were, because I was performing my song stylings.  ``That was my life,'' she adds. ``Whatever else I did, music was my mission. The other things were relatively unimportant, if not really quite a bore.''  It was sheer coincidence that documentary filmmaker Bert Stern filmed her performance at the 1958 Newport Jazz Festival for his classic documentary, ``Jazz on a Summer's Day,'' but it was a tremendous boost.  O'Day's sinuous, sensuously discombobulated version of ``Sweet Georgia Brown'' and her lightning ``Tea for Two'' proved a watershed in her career, exposing her to a much wider audience.  Perhaps if she had been more ambitious _ and if she hadn't been in the midst of a 14-year struggle against heroin addition _ O'Day might have parlayed the film's success into a high-profile career to rival Fitzgerald's or Vaughan's.  Perhaps, but probably not. Some may say that she was held back by being a white woman in a black art form, but she never competed with black vocalists, never adopted the style or techniques of black jazz.  The fact is, Anita O'Day never wanted to be a star. To the extent that she became one, it was an accident, a side effect of her real passion. She had nothing to spare from singing for the pursuit of stardom.  ``I put a lot of work in it,'' she says. ``At the club, every night, trying this, trying that. I don't have any kids, don't have a husband.  ``And that's the price you pay, but I took it,'' she says, coolly and without regrets. ``That's what I wanted.'' c.1997 Leonce Gaiter  (Leonce Gaiter is a Los Angeles-based free-lance writer.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) ------------------------------------------------- </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19970113.0221 type=story > <HEADLINE> INVESTORS POUR MONEY IN U.S. STOCK FUNDS AFTER DEC. (The New York Times)  (NYT7) CAMBRIDGE, England -- Jan. 21, 2002 -- SCI-BLACKHOLES-3 -- In the fall of 1973, Dr. Stephen Hawking who has spent his entire professional career at the University of Cambridge in England, discovered to his disbelief that black holes could leak energy and particles into space, and even explode in a fountain of high-energy sparks. Nearly 30 years later, at a symposium to mark Hawking's 60th birthday, his ideas that touch on just about every concept in modern physics often took center stage. The gathering to celebrate Hawking's 60th birthday drew leading theoretical physicists, including, from left, Dr. Andrew Strominger of Harvard University and Dr. Raffael Bousso of the University of California at Santa Barbara.
