(Jonathan Player/The New York Times)  (NYT4) DURHAM, N.C. -- Jan. 21, 2002 -- SCI-HEART-STRESS -- Teaching cardiac patients how to manage stress may be as effective as aerobic exercise in decreasing the long-term risk of further coronary illness, and it may also reduce medical costs, researchers at Duke University Medical Center in Durham, N.C. have found. The Duke study, which followed heart patients for five years, is believed to be the first to measure the benefits of stress management over a sustained period and to demonstrate its potential economic benefits. Dr. James Blumenthal of Duke, right, found that managing stress helped heart patients, including Dan Saldana, left, who completed stress management training. (Jenny Warburg/The New York Times)  (NYT3) PHILADELPHIA -- Jan. 21, 2002 -- SCI-BLUMBERG-ASTROBIOLOGY -- Now, at 76, Dr. Baruch S. Blumberg, a Nobel laureate, is in the prime of his second scientific career. In the 1960s, Blumberg worked with a team of researchers trying to discover why different groups of people had different levels of resistance to various diseases. Then, three years ago, Blumberg was tapped to direct NASA's Astrobiology Institute. Blumberg poses outside the Fox Chase Cancer Center in Philadelphia. (Sal DiMarco/The New York Times)  (NYT2) UNDATED -- Jan. 21, 2002 -- SCI-FOSSILS-OCEAN-CURRENTS -- Dr. Ellen E. Martin, a professor of geological sciences at the University of Florida, believes the teeth of long-dead fish in the Pacific Ocean could provide an answer about a sweeping shift of deep ocean currents millions of years ago. The teeth preserve a record of the changing chemical composition of the ocean water. Fossil fish teeth, like this one greatly magnified, are actually about one-fortieth of an inch long. (Ellen E. Martin/University of Florida/The New York Times)  (NYT1) UNDATED -- Jan. 21, 2002 -- SCI-INFLAMMATION-DRUG -- Dutch researchers found that people who took anti-inflammatory drugs like ibuprofen for at least two years were only one-sixth as likely to get Alzheimer's as people who did not take the drugs. The studies reflect scientists' growing interest in the idea that a common condition, inflammation, may underlie many chronic and debilitating diseases and that drugs that fight inflammation may have a role in preventing or delaying those diseases, or at least slowing them down. Tylenol treats pain and fever, but doesn't have the same anti-inflammatory properties of drugs like Motrin and Advil that may play a role in fighting debilitating diseases. TOP  Lead story:  1-col. hed:  TERROR-INQUIRY (Washington) _ President Bush on Wednesday named Henry A. Kissinger, a Republican who has been one of the most esteemed but polarizing figures in foreign policy and Washington for more than three decades, to lead an independent investigation into the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks. Bush made the appointment as he signed legislation creating the commission, a step he came to support only after opposing the bill for much of the year on the grounds that it could divert attention from the war on terrorism. Democratic leaders in Congress, who will appoint half the members of the 10-person commission, immediately named George Mitchell, the former Senate majority leader and peace envoy to Northern Ireland and the Middle East, as vice chairman. FOLD  IRAQ (United Nations) _ U.N. arms inspectors on Wednesday made their first field forays in Iraq in four years, checking three sites outside Baghdad with no reported resistance from Iraqi officials. The inspections resumed in accordance with a Security Council resolution adopted unanimously on Nov. 8, which essentially gives President Saddam Hussein of Iraq a last chance to cooperate with arms inspectors and to give a full accounting of his weapons of mass destruction. By Serge Schmemann.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in ``i'' news file.) Turkey's agreement is key to the Bush administration's plan to open a northern front if there is a military confrontation with Iraq. By Michael R. Gordon.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in ``i'' news file.)  FDA-ALLERGY-DRUG (Undated) _ Federal regulators on Wednesday approved the nation's top-selling allergy drug, Claritin, as an over-the-counter medicine, a decision with wide implications for patients, doctors, insurers and drug companies. Nonprescription sales of Claritin will begin next month. Experts said that the change will mean big savings for people without insurance but could raise prices for the insured, whose purchases of Claritin will no longer be subsidized. By Melody Petersen.  With a graphic. But from Boston to Atlanta to Los Angeles, passengers said they were finding something quite unexpected in their efforts to reach friends and family in a distant city: a pleasant experience. By Michael Janofsky.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  BOTTOM  MEXICO-U.S.-TRUCKS (Washington) _ President Bush gave the go-ahead Wednesday for Mexican trucks to travel U.S. roads beyond commercial border zones where they have been restricted while inspection sites and new regulations were put in place. Articles from Part One, budgeted below, will move beginning Monday, Dec. 16, and throughout the week, for immediate release. Part Two articles will move shortly after the beginning of the year and will be the subject of a separate budget.  All articles will carry a slug of the form BC-OUTLOOK-slug and carry an ``f'' financial category code.  OUTLOOK-CONFIDENCE (Undated) _ What will it take to restore confidence and trust in the markets, management, corporations, accountants, technology, and the United States as a global leader in this post-scandal, post-Enron business environment? Economic growth, of course. Even so, these are not normal times, given terrorism and the threat of war. What will be the engine of economic growth? Can anything take the place of technology? Even with the tumult wrought by Enron, Andersen, WorldCom, Tyco and others in 2002, how much has really changed? By Alex Berenson.  OUTLOOK-SCANDALS (Undated) _ It was, it seemed, the year when corporate America went crooked as it never had before, when chicanery took the place of integrity, when wrongdoing exploded in ways that could not have been anticipated. But then again, maybe it wasn't. Despite all the surprise and anguish set off in the past year by corporate scandals, for many Wall Street historians and fraud aficionados, it almost fit into a pattern _ the re-emergence of a moment that has become something of a tradition in the financial world. Most everyone knows of the business cycle of boom and busts, but what is less well known is capitalism has a cycle of scandal as well. By Kurt Eichenwald.  With photo.  OUTLOOK-SCANDALS-PAST (Undated) _ A historian recalls other big scandals. By Charles P. Kindleberger.  With photo.  OUTLOOK-OVERSIGHT (Undated) _ Is the SEC (post-Pitt) and the new accounting board up to the task? By Stephen Labaton.  OUTLOOK-AMERICAN-DREAM (Undated) _ Is it the American Dream, or Wretched Excess? Signs of the '00s: Soaring executive compensation (including stock profits). 60,000-square-foot houses. The Hummer. The $10 million bra from Victoria's Secret. Oversized portions. Pop-up ads. A cynical examination of the post-entrepreneurial era. Has the American Dream been reduced to these kinds of things? By John Schwartz.  With photo.  OUTLOOK-TAXES (Undated) _ The many coming tax issues in a Republican-dominated year. By David Cay Johnston.  OUTLOOK-AIRLINES (Undated) _ Airlines are in a world of hurt. By Micheline Maynard.  OUTLOOK-BUSH-ECONOMY (Washington) _ What are President Bush's priorities? By Edmund L. Andrews.  OUTLOOK-AUTOS (Undated) _ Automakers face a capacity glut. By Danny Hakim. With photo.  OUTLOOK-CONSUMERS (Undated) _ An analysis of the balance sheet of consumers, encompassing debt and cash flows. By David Leonhardt.  With graphic.  OUTLOOK-GLOBAL (Undated) _ As the world's business leader, the United States has stumbled. Can it right itself? Can anyone take its place? A look at the problems of an unbalanced global economy. By Daniel Altman.  With graphic.  OUTLOOK-EUROPE (Frankfurt, Germany) _ If one were to judge Europe's fledgling monetary union by its ability to generate economic growth in member countries, one would have to judge it a keen disappointment. Still, Europe's transition to a monetary union has imposed unique restrictions on governments, tying them to a regionwide monetary policy and a set of fiscal guidelines that require that they keep their deficits below a ceiling and balance their budgets by 2006. By Mark Landler.  OUTLOOK-CHINA (Shanghai) _ China's economy has defied the worldwide slowdown and continued its long streak of rapid growth, raising expectations that it has begun to pull its weight as an economic engine for Asia and the world. By Joseph Kahn.  OUTLOOK-JAPAN (Tokyo) _ Japan will remain the world's second-largest economy for at least two decades, but it will not be the locomotive for a world recovery next year. By James Brooke.  OUTLOOK-RUSSIA (Moscow) _ Russia's economy is entering its fifth year of economic growth, a milestone that would have been unthinkable during the chaotic, economic free-for-all of Boris Yeltsin's Russia. Companies have begun to restructure, the government's economic reforms have begun to take hold, and capital flight has been steadily falling. But even with this running start, it could prove difficult for the country to overcome a sharp drop in the price of oil, the single-largest source of revenue for the Russian budget. By Sabrina Tavernise.  With photo.  OUTLOOK-INDIA (New Delhi, India) _ Focusing on the state of Hyderabad, looking at what the state government has done in the way of public policy to make a boom there possible, along with lessons for the rest of India. By Keith Bradsher.  OUTLOOK-MEDIA (Undated) _ Will 2003 be a better year for the media big shot? The Viacom leadership should be resolved by year-end. Jean-Rene Fourtou of Vivendi should be making progress selling off assets. With the possible exception of Barry Diller and Howard Stringer, 2002 wasn't the best of years for media moguls. Michael Eisner is under the gun, Steve Case is under scrutiny, John Rigas is under indictment, Jean-Marie Messier is out of work. By Geraldine Fabrikant.  With photo.  OUTLOOK-MUSIC (Undated) _ Can the music industry recover? By Laura M. Holson.  With graphics.  OUTLOOK-ADVERTISING (Undated) _ After a bumpy year in advertising, better times are predicted, with TV spending the standout. By Stuart Elliott.  OUTLOOK-BIOTECH (Undated)_ The FDA's stance on drug approvals has hurt the biotechnology industry. In 2003, biotech drug applications are being shifted to another division in the agency. Will this hurt or help? By Andrew Pollack.  OUTLOOK-NANOTECH (Undated) _ The Next Big Thing for technology may be nano-sized. By Barnaby J. Feder.  With graphic.  OUTLOOK-MAGAZINES (Undated) _ After the shakeout of 2002, magazines may have lost something they can't get back in advertising market share. Have marketers rethought their overall magazine advertising strategy? How long will it take for magazines to recover? By David Carr.  OUTLOOK-BOOKS (Undated) _ Television morning shows have stepped into the void left by Oprah after she stopped her book club. And there is evidence that book sales are starting to rise. By Bill Goldstein.  OUTLOOK-TELECOMS (Undated) _ When and where might the telecom recovery come? What companies are in the best position to benefit - or at least survive? REALITY-TV-LAWSUITS (Undated) _ James and Laurie Ann Ryan's Las Vegas vacation was more exciting than they would have liked. "138223"	"it"	"seemed"	" For Use By Clients of the New York Times News Service </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20030826.0047 type=advis > <DATELINE> 08-26-03 18:52:00EDT (BC-EDITORIAL-SAMPRAS-BOS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> It's almost impossible to visualize the game of professional tennis without Pete Sampras, and a lot of people won't even try.  Sampras defined the sport for the past decade, dominating it since 1990 when he won his first US Open at age 19 _ the youngestm!de player to do so. He went on to take four more US Open titles as well as two Australian Open victories and seven Wimbledon trophies for a total of 14 grand slam championships, more than any other male singles player in the history of the game.  Such a giant lingers forever in the heart of the fan and looms over the swinging rackets of all players, top-ranked or rank amateur. Sampras, 32, may have called it a career Monday at the start of this year's US Open, but his presence will be felt on the tour as powerfully as if his name were still in the draw.  Everybody with a huge serve will be measured against him _ particularly the 20-year-old American player Andy Roddick, who is said to be ``the next Pete,'' though 21-year-old Roger Federer of Switzerland also became an heir apparent after winning Wimbledon.  None of the new guys has achieved the stats yet, however, nor the gravitas of the hulking Sampras taking the court. He often kept his face expressionless and slack-jawed, mouth open, so a viewer might worry about him swallowing bugs. Sometimes he seemed to be shambling back to the base line looking a little hang-dog after losing a point, the body language masking the power that was about to blast an opponent's rising confidenkefack down into the fear zone.  He rarely appreared nervous on big points, dispatching them quickly and, it seemed, easily, without the grunts, groans, grimaces or death grip on the racket often seen in other players. He was criticized for lacking a show-biz personality and for going about his business so matter-of-factly and with such consistency that his wins began to seem routine: He was ranked top in the world for a record six consecutive years, and he got there with none of the crowd-pleasing histrionics of a John McEnroe or Jimmy Connors.  He won games with pulled muscles, sore feet, and cramps. He battled nausea so fierce he vomited between points as he beat Alex Corretja of Spain in the quarterfinal of the 1996 US Open. He sobbed through a match in the 1995 Australian Open after learning that his coach, Tim Gullikson, was dying of cancer _ and he won the match.  Standing in New York's Arthur Ashe Stadium Monday night, he let the rare public tears flow again and said he knew in his heart it was time to leave the game. It is the heart, after all, that ultimately makes a champion; talent is just one part of the equation. The N.Y. Times News Service night supervisor is Lynn Hoogenboom; phone: (888) 346-9867; e-mail: lyhoognytimes.com.  INTERNATIONAL  (Will move in i news file.)  IRAQ (Baghdad, Iraq) -- Insurgents penetrated the American-controlled Green Zone on Thursday, setting off a pair of bombs within seconds of each other and killing five people, including three Americans, and wounding 20 others. Witnesses said that at least one of the explosions was set off by a suicide bomber and that the second may have been as well. Neither American nor Iraqi government officials had any immediate explanation as to how the bombs were smuggled inside. By Dexter Filkins.  PRISONER-ABUSE (Washington) -- A just-completed Army criminal investigation has implicated 28 active-duty and reserve personnel in the homicides of two Afghan men detained at the American air base at Bagram in December 2002, and describes potential offenses ranging from involuntary manslaughter to assault to conspiracy, the Army said on Thursday. By Thom Shanker.  MIDEAST (Jerusalem) -- Prime Minister Ariel Sharon said on Thursday that he wanted to begin withdrawing Israeli settlers from the Gaza Strip next May or June and complete the pullout within three months. Sharon's comments to a closed session of parliament's Defense and Foreign Affairs Committee marked the most specific target date he has given for the Gaza evacuation. The Israeli media reported the remarks, which also were confirmed by participants at the session. However, Sharon must still win approval for the plan in parliament, and it is scheduled to come up for debate and a vote on Oct. 25. By Greg Myre.  MALARIA-VACCINE (Undated) -- For the first time, researchers say, a vaccine against malaria has shown that it can save children from infection or death. The vaccine, tested on thousands of children in Mozambique, was hardly perfect: It protected them from catching the disease only about 30 percent of the time and prevented it from becoming life-threatening only about 58 percent of the time. But because malaria kills over a million people a year, 700,000 of them children, even partial protection would be a public health victory. By Donald G. McNeil Jr.  NATIONAL GENERAL  (Will move in a news file.)  FLU-VACCINE (Undated) -- Although President Bush said the country was talking to Canada about obtaining more flu vaccine, it's unlikely that there will be much additional vaccine available this year, federal health officials said Thursday. By Andrew Pollack.  POLITICS  (Will move in p news file.)  CAMPAIGN-RDP (Central Point, Ore.) -- President Bush and Sen. John Kerry stormed back onto the campaign trail on Thursday only hours after their last debate created what both sides said was an essentially new race that after hundreds of millions of dollars and nearly a year of campaigning had come down to a tie in the last 19 days. By Elisabeth Bumiller and David M. Halbfinger.  DEBATES (Phoenix, Ariz.) -- You might call it a flip-flop -- that is, if you even remember the term. In the debates, it was Sen. John Kerry who proved almost robotically consistent, affecting the same earnest, respectful demeanor even as, through each debate, he lacerated President Bush as incompetent in fighting a war and helping the middle class. It was Bush who was changeable, shifting the thrust of his attacks -- from saying that Kerry was inconstant to saying he was constantly liberal -- and even, it seemed, adjusting his personality for, of all things, political expediency. Yet it was also Kerry who had the most to accomplish in the debates By James Bennet and Jim Rutenberg.  FINANCIAL  (Will move in f' news file.)  INSURE-FRAUD (New York) -- Eliot Spitzer, the New York state attorney general, accused the world's largest insurance broker on Thursday of cheating customers through a long-standing arrangement of rigging prices and steering business to insurance companies in exchange for lucrative kick-backs. By Joseph B. Treaster.  SPORTS  (Will move in s news file.) MIDEAST (Jerusalem) -- Prime Minister Ariel Sharon of Israel suggests he may delay the Gaza withdrawal by up to three weeks. Other developments. By Greg Myre.  BOLTON-CONFIRM (Washington) -- Developments on the nomination of John R. Bolton to be U.S. ambassador to the United Nations. The committee vote on his confirmation is planned for Tuesday. By Douglas Jehl and Steven R. Weisman.  ITALY-POLITICS (Rome) -- Prime minister Silvio Berlusconi was expected to formally hand in his resignation today and was due to present a reshuffled cabinet that he hopes will permit him to stay in office until his mandate runs out in 2006. His action was designed to resolve one of the worst crises of his four-year tenure, which began last week when a rebellious coalition ally pulled four ministers from the center-right government. By Elisabetta Povoledo.  MEXICO-MAYOR (Mexico City) -- Developments in the prosecution of Mexico City's popular mayor, Andres Manuel Lopez Obrador, which has created a political crisis in the country. On Monday President Vicente Fox's spokesman said there was no way he would pardon the mayor because the Mexico constitution forbids it. By Ginger Thompson and James C. McKinley Jr.  ECUADOR-POLITICS (Quito, Ecuador) -- Developments in the political crisis in Ecuador that threatens President Lucio Gutierrez because of what he calls his struggle to created a corruption-free judiciary. By Juan Forero.  CANADA-POLITCS (Toronto) A look at how Prime Minister Paul Martin is scrambling to keep power amid a growing Liberal Party scandal, making deals that delay or even jettison central elements of his political agenda, including a bill to legalize same-sex marriages and a measure to control greenhouse gases in Canada. By Clifford Kruass.  BASQUES-ELECT (Dateline TK, Spain) -- Folo on weekend elections in Spain's Basque country, which weakened the influence of the separatists. By Renwick McLean.  UNITED STATES -- National General (a code)  MINN-SHOOTING (Dateline) -- Developments in the Red Lake Indian reservation school shootings. By Monica Davey.  JACKSON-TRIAL (Santa Maria, Calif.) -- Prosecution testimony continues in the Michael Jackson child molestation trial. By Nick Madigan.  UNITED STATES -- Washington (w code)  SCOTUS (Washington) -- Supreme Court Roundup: The Supreme Court accepts a case in which the question is whether a religious group in New Mexico that uses hallucinogenic tea in its religious services has the right as a matter of freedom to import the substance. The tea contains a drug that is banned under the federal Controlled Substances Act. The Bush administration says that makes the tea illegal and possibly dangerous for members of the church. The administration lost in the lower courts and will now will try to win on appeal. By Linda Greenhouse.  FINANCIAL (f code)  MARKET (Undated) -- Trading on U.S. equity and financial markets on Monday as stocks fell sharply in Asia, with the Nikkei down 4 percent, and were lower in Europe. By Jonathan Fuerbringer.  DRUG-PATENTS (Undated) -- The Supreme Court will hear arguments on Wednesday in a drug industry patent case that could have broad implications for medical research and, as a result, has made temporary allies of various corporate and consumer groups that more typically find themselves on opposite sides of any issue. The case pits Merck against Integra Lifesciences, a small New Jersey biotechnology company. Integra contends that researchers backed by Merck used Integra's patented molecules without authorization in early-stage drug development. Merck argues that such use is legal under a 1984 law that allows patent infringement in some cases if scientists are preparing information to present to the FDA. By Andrew Pollack.  BATHROOM-TECH (Undated) -- At American Standard, the world's largest bathroom and kitchen fixtures company, the physics of flushing and the properties of ceramics are deadly serious. American Standard has promised consumers that the Champion, which uses a complicated flushing tower instead of the familiar flapper and chains, will never clog, no way, no how. Just as important, Champion could be the source of a welcome image change. Bathroom and kitchen fixtures represented just about a quarter of American Standard's $9.5 billion in sales last year; its Trane air conditioners are its biggest sellers, and its Wabco vehicle braking systems are its most profitable products. But when consumers free-associate to the American Standard name, cheap, utilitarian bathrooms are what come to mind. Analysts say the Champion -- which lists for $408, although some stores sell it for $250 -- may yet make the company's name synonymous with upscale products that consumers crave. By Claudia H. Deutsch.  CHINA-ENERGY (Guangzhou, China) -- The clash between China's Communist past and its increasingly capitalist present is now playing out in this country's energy markets, causing fuel shortages and making electricity blackouts temporarily worse. Tight regulations on retail prices for diesel and electricity, a legacy of central planning, have made increasingly profit-oriented refiners and power companies reluctant to sell either. Global markets set wholesale prices for diesel and for the heavy fuel oil used by power stations, but the government still insists on setting retail prices. Disruptions in Chinese markets for these and other oil products are causing ripples in global markets in turn, as traders and investors around the world struggle to interpret the effects on international oil supply and demand. By Keith Bradsher.  INSURE-HOULDSWORTH (Killiney, Ireland) -- A profile of John Houldsworth, the former chief executive of Cologne Re, who organized the transaction with the American International Group that is the center of an investigation into the company. By Brian Lavery.  SINGAPORE-CASINOS (Singapore) -- Singapore said it would legalize casinos to allow the construction of two Las Vegas-style resorts, overruling public opposition in an effort to boost tourism and temper its famous Puritanism with a willingness to gamble on innovation. Singapore plans to award licenses to build the two casinos, which it estimates will entail roughly $3 billion in investment, by the end of next year. The hope is that introducing casinos will enable Singapore to attract more tourists from mainland China. More broadly, however, the casinos are part of a longstanding effort to reorient Singapore's economy toward higher value-added service industries such as tourism and biosciences as it loses manufacturing competitiveness to China. By Wayne Arnold.  JAPAN-MEDIA (Tokyo) -- A contentious and closely watched Japanese takeover battle ended amicably when the Internet start-up Livedoor agreed to sell most of its stake in the target company, Nippon Broadcasting, to rival bidder Fuji Television Network Inc. Under the agreement, Fuji TV will also take a 15 percent stake in Livedoor to seal what the companies say is now a friendly alliance. By Todd Zaun.  ADOBE-MACROMEDIA (Undated) -- Adobe Systems agreed to buy Macromedia for about $3.4 billion in stock to extend its lead in the market for creating and distributing digital documents. By Laurie J. Flynn. With a photo.  VIDEO-GAME-LANGUAGE (Undated) -- The language Tho Fan sounds ancient and distinctly Asian. Its sh sounds come from the back of the throat, as they do in Chinese. Its r sounds are made with a tap of the tongue, echoing Mongolian. But Tho Fan comes from Canada and was invented only last year. It is a real language spoken by unreal people in the Xbox game Jade Empire, released this week. Perhaps it is a sign that, these days, languages are not so much discovered as invented. By Stephen Totilo.  LIGHT-THEATER-REVIEW (New York) -- Ben Brantley reviews The Light in the Piazza: at the Vivian Beaumont.  SHADOWS-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) -- Cast of Shadows, by Kevin Guilefoil (Knopf). Reviewed by Michiko Kakutani. PORTS-DUBAI (Washington) -- The Bush administration is seeking a way to delay the formal acquisition of leases of six major port terminals by a Dubai company to give the White house time to assuage angry Republicans and Democrats in Congress worried about the security implications of the deal, administration officials said on Thursday. The tentative moves by the White House came as Bush administration officials and Senate Democrats clashed in a public hearing on Capitol Hill over the national security risks of allowing a Dubai company to run operations at major American ports. By David S. Cloud and David E. Sanger.  With photo.  FANNIE-MAE (Washington) -- Investigators have uncovered new evidence that senior executives of Fannie Mae, the nation's largest purchaser of home mortgages, manipulated its accounting in the 1990s to meet earnings projections so that top executives could get more than $27 million in bonuses. In a 2,600-page report concluding an 18 month investigation, former Sen. Warren Rudman and a team of lawyers and investigators concluded that Fannie Mae's accounting practices in virtually all of the areas that we reviewed were not consistent with generally accepted accounting principles. By Stephen Labaton and Eric Dash.  With a graphic.  TWINS-SCHOOL (Undated) -- Parents of twins and triplets are challenging long-standing -- and, they say, outmoded -- educational practices that require their children be separated in the classroom, in part to foster their independence. The passionate debate is polarizing families and school administrators across the country, and drawing the attention of lawmakers who say cases should be evaluated on an individual family basis. Advocates of parental choice point to studies in the past two years that have suggested that twins, for example, may actually benefit from being left together in their early years. Since last year, when Minnesota became the first state to allow parents to decide how children of multiple births will be educated, similar legislation has been introduced in Illinois, with groups in New York, Texas, Massachusetts and North Carolina working to get such statutes introduced. By Ginia Bellafante.  KATRINA-DEBRIS (Ponchatoula, La.) -- Dozens of courthouse wrangles and public controversies have erupted recently over the still-gargantuan task of removing tons of debris in Louisiana and Mississippi. The problems are now so clear that even the Department of Homeland Security and its congressional critics said last week that the entire process for cleaning up after storms -- and paying for that cleanup -- needs to be restructured. Contractors and workers, ranging from individual laborers to a quality-control consulting firm, claim that they have been abused, underpaid, or not paid at all. Local politicians are fuming about the slow pace of cleanup and their lack of control over what happens in their communities. And government investigators have opened at least five probes into debris removal operations, and federal prosecutors have filed two criminal complaints involving debris removal. By Leslie Eaton.
